	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >73rd United States Congress</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">1933–1935 U.S. Congress</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox vevent" style="width:25em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above">73rd United States Congress</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader"><div style="float: Rainey">Henry T. Rainey</a> (D) <br /> (until August 19, 1934)</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header"><a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Congresses" title="List of United States Congresses">Sessions</a></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="white-space: nowrap;"><b>Special</b>: March 4, 1933 – March 6, 1933<br /><b>1st</b>: March 9, 1933&#160;– June 15, 1933<br /><b>2nd</b>: January 3, 1934 – June 18, 1934</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>73rd United States Congress</b> was a meeting of the legislative branch of the United States federal government, composed of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>. It met in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> from March 4, 1933, to January 3, 1935, during the first two years of <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Presidency of Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt's presidency</a>. Because of the newly ratified <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Twentieth amendment to the United States Constitution">20th Amendment</a>, the duration of this Congress, along with the term of office of those elected to it, was shortened by the interval between January 3 and March 4, 1935 (61 days). The apportionment of seats in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> was based on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_1930" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Census, 1930">Fifteenth Census of the United States in 1930</a>. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a> greatly increased their majority in the House, and won control of the Senate for the first time since the <a href="/wiki/65th_United_States_Congress" title="65th United States Congress">65th Congress</a> in 1917. With <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> being sworn in a <a href="/wiki/U.S._President" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. President">President</a> on March 4, 1933, this gave the Democrats an overall federal government <a href="/wiki/Government_trifecta#United_States" title="Government trifecta">trifecta</a>, also for the first time since the 65th Congress.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_events">Major events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Major events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/1933_in_the_United_States" title="1933 in the United States">1933 in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/1934_in_the_United_States" title="1934 in the United States">1934 in the United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1935_in_the_United_States" title="1935 in the United States">1935 in the United States</a></div>
<ul><li>March 4, 1933: <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> became <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a></li>
<li>January 3, 1934: The second session of 73rd Congress convened as mandated by the <a href="/wiki/Twentieth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twentieth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twentieth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>, that had been <a href="/wiki/Ratified" class="mw-redirect" title="Ratified">ratified</a> one year earlier</li>
<li>August 19, 1934: <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Thomas_Rainey" title="Henry Thomas Rainey">Henry Thomas Rainey</a> died of a heart attack.  The House had already completed its work for this Congress and had already adjourned.  No Speaker was elected until the next Congress.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_legislation">Major legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Major legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_federal_legislation#73rd_United_States_Congress" title="List of United States federal legislation">List of United States federal legislation §&#160;73rd United States Congress</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Session">First Session</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First Session">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first session of Congress, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Days Congress">Hundred Days</a>", took place before the regular seating and was called by President Roosevelt specifically to pass two acts:
</p>
<ul><li>March 9, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Banking_Act" title="Emergency Banking Act">Emergency Banking Act</a> (ch. 1, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-1">1</a>) was enacted within four hours of its introduction. It was prompted by the "<a href="/wiki/Bank_holiday" title="Bank holiday">bank holiday</a>" and was the first step in Roosevelt's "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred Days Congress">first hundred days</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. The Act was drafted in large part by officials appointed by the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Hoover</a> administration. The bill provided for the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Treasury_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Treasury Department">Treasury Department</a> to initiate <a href="/wiki/Reserve_requirement" title="Reserve requirement">reserve requirements</a> and a federal bailout to large failing institutions. It also removed the United States from the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Gold Standard">Gold Standard</a>. All <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">banks</a> had to undergo a federal inspection to deem if they were stable enough to re-open. Within a week 1/3 of the banks re-opened in the United States and faith was, in large part, restored in the banking system. The act had few opponents, only taking fire from the farthest left elements of Congress who wanted to <a href="/wiki/Nationalize" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalize">nationalize</a> banks altogether.</li>
<li>March 10, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Economy_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Economy Act">Economy Act of 1933</a>. Roosevelt, in sending this act to Congress, warned that if it did not pass, the country faced a billion dollar <a href="/wiki/Government_budget_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Government budget deficit">deficit</a>. The act balanced the federal budget by cutting the salaries of government employees and cutting pensions to veterans by as much as 15 percent. It intended to reassure the deficit hawks that the new president was fiscally conservative. Although the act was heavily protested by left-leaning members of congress, it passed by an overwhelming margin.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_(1933).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg/220px-Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg/330px-Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg/440px-Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_%281933%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2155" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roosevelt_signing_TVA_Act_(1933).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Franklin D. Roosevelt signs the Tennessee Valley Authority Act</div></div></div>
<p>The session also passed several other major pieces of legislation:
</p>
<ul><li>March 31, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps_Reforestation_Relief_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Civilian Conservation Corps Reforestation Relief Act">Civilian Conservation Corps Reforestation Relief Act</a> (ch. 17, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-22">22</a>) established the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> (CCC) as a means to combat <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>.</li>
<li>May 12, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Adjustment_Act" title="Agricultural Adjustment Act">Agricultural Adjustment Act</a> (ch. 25, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-31">31</a>) was part of a plan developed by Roosevelt's <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Secretary of Agriculture">Secretary of Agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_A._Wallace" title="Henry A. Wallace">Henry A. Wallace</a>, and was designed to protect American <a href="/wiki/Farmers" class="mw-redirect" title="Farmers">farmers</a> from the uncertainties of the depression through subsidies and production controls. The act laid the frame for long-term government control in the planning of the agricultural sector. In 1936 the act was ruled unconstitutional by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> because it taxed one group to pay for another.</li>
<li>May 12, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Relief_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Emergency Relief Act">Federal Emergency Relief Act</a> (ch. 30, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-55">55</a>) established the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Relief_Administration" title="Federal Emergency Relief Administration">Federal Emergency Relief Administration</a> (FERA) which develop public works projects to give work to the unemployed.</li>
<li>May 18, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley_Authority" title="Tennessee Valley Authority">Tennessee Valley Authority Act</a> (ch. 32, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-58">58</a>) created the Tennessee Valley Authority to relieve the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee_Valley" title="Tennessee Valley">Tennessee Valley</a> by a series of public works projects.</li>
<li>June 5, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Securities_Act_of_1933" title="Securities Act of 1933">Securities Act of 1933</a> (ch. 38, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-74">74</a>) established the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) as a way for the government to prevent a repeat of the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_1929" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Crash 1929">Stock Market Crash of 1929</a>.</li>
<li>June 12, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Act_of_1933" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass–Steagall Act of 1933">Glass–Steagall Act of 1933</a> (ch. 89, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-162">162</a>) was a follow up to the <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Act_of_1932" title="Glass–Steagall Act of 1932">Glass–Steagall Act of 1932</a>. Both acts sought to make banking safer and less prone to speculation. The 1933 act, however, established the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a>.</li>
<li>June 16, 1933: The <a href="/wiki/National_Industrial_Recovery_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="National Industrial Recovery Act">National Industrial Recovery Act</a> ("NIRA", ch. 90, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-195">195</a>) was an anti-<a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a> scheme promoted by the <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="Chamber of Commerce">Chamber of Commerce</a> that reversed <a href="/wiki/Anti-trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-trust">anti-trust</a> laws and permit trade associations to cooperate in stabilizing prices within their industries while making businesses ensure that the incomes of workers would rise along with their prices. It guaranteed to workers of the right of collective bargaining and helped spur major union organizing drives in major industries. In case consumer buying power lagged behind, thereby defeating the administration's initiatives, the NIRA created the <a href="/wiki/Public_Works_Administration" title="Public Works Administration">Public Works Administration</a> (PWA), a major program of public works spending designed to alleviate unemployment, and moreover to transfer funds to certain beneficiaries. The NIRA established the most important, but ultimately least successful provision: a new federal agency known as the <a href="/wiki/National_Recovery_Administration" title="National Recovery Administration">National Recovery Administration</a> (NRA), which attempted to stabilize prices and wages through cooperative "code authorities" involving government, business, and labor. The NIRA was seen hailed as a miracle, responding to the needs of labor, business, unemployment, and the deflation crisis. The "<a href="/wiki/A.L.A._Schechter_Poultry_Corp._v._United_States" title="A.L.A. Schechter Poultry Corp. v. United States">sick chicken case</a>" led to the Supreme Court invalidating NIRA in 1935.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Session">Second Session</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Second Session">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>March 24, 1934: The <a href="/wiki/Tydings%E2%80%93McDuffie_Act" title="Tydings–McDuffie Act">Tydings–McDuffie Act</a> (<a href="/wiki/Public_Law_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Law (United States)"><abbr title="Public Law (United States)">Pub.L.</abbr></a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://uslaw.link/citation/us-law/public/73/127">73–127</a>, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-456">456</a>) provided for self-government for the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_the_Philippines" title="Commonwealth of the Philippines">Commonwealth of the Philippines</a> and a pathway to independence.</li>
<li>June 6, 1934: The <a href="/wiki/Securities_Exchange_Act_of_1934" title="Securities Exchange Act of 1934">Securities Exchange Act of 1934</a> (ch. 404, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-881">881</a>) grew out of the <a href="/wiki/Securities_Act_of_1933" title="Securities Act of 1933">Securities Act of 1933</a> and regulated participation in financial markets.</li>
<li>June 6, 1934: The <a href="/wiki/National_Firearms_Act_of_1934" class="mw-redirect" title="National Firearms Act of 1934">National Firearms Act of 1934</a> (ch. 757, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-1236">1236</a>) regulated machine guns, short-barreled rifles and shotguns.</li>
<li>June 19, 1934: <a href="/wiki/Communications_Act_of_1934" title="Communications Act of 1934">Communications Act of 1934</a> (ch. 652, 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-48-1064">1064</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_Law_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Law (United States)"><abbr title="Public Law (United States)">Pub.L.</abbr></a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://uslaw.link/citation/us-law/public/73/416">73–416</a>)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_amendments">Constitutional amendments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Constitutional amendments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>December 5, 1933: <a href="/wiki/Twenty-first_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-first Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twenty-first Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>, repealing the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Eighteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">eighteenth amendment</a> and thus ending <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">prohibition in the United States</a>, was ratified by the requisite number of states (then 36) to become part of the Constitution<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hearings">Hearings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Hearings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id=".22Merchants_of_Death.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Merchants_of_Death&quot;">"Merchants of Death"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: &quot;Merchants of Death&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><b>Committee</b>: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Special_Committee_on_Investigation_of_the_Munitions_Industry" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate Special Committee on Investigation of the Munitions Industry">United States Senate Special Committee on Investigation of the Munitions Industry</a></li>
<li><b>Chairman</b>: Senator <a href="/wiki/Gerald_P._Nye" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald P. Nye">Gerald P. Nye</a> (R)</li>
<li><b>Duration</b>: September 4, 1934 – February 24, 1936</li></ul>
<p>The Senate Munitions Committee came into existence solely for the purpose of this hearing. Although World War I had been over for sixteen years, there were revived reports that America's leading munition companies had effectively influenced the United States into that conflict, which killed 53,000 Americans, hence the companies' nickname "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Merchants_of_Death&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Merchants of Death (page does not exist)">Merchants of Death</a>".
</p><p>The Democratic Party, controlling the Senate for the first time since the first world war, used the hype of these reports to organize the hearing in hopes of <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalizing</a> America's munitions industry. The Democrats chose a Republican renowned for his ardent <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">isolationist</a> policies, Senator Gerald P. Nye of North Dakota, to head the hearing. Nye was typical of <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">western</a> <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Progressivism" title="Progressivism">progressives</a>, and adamantly opposed America's involvement in any foreign war. Nye declared at the opening of the hearing "when the Senate investigation is over, we shall see that war and preparation for war is not a matter of national honor and national defense, but a matter of profit for the few."
</p><p>Over the next eighteen months, the "<a href="/wiki/Nye_Committee" title="Nye Committee">Nye Committee</a>" (as <a href="/wiki/Newspapers" class="mw-redirect" title="Newspapers">newspapers</a> called it) held ninety-three hearings, questioning more than two hundred witnesses, including <a href="/wiki/J.P._Morgan,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="J.P. Morgan, Jr.">J.P. Morgan, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_S._du_Pont" title="Pierre S. du Pont">Pierre du Pont</a>. Committee members found little hard evidence of an active conspiracy among arms makers, yet the panel's reports did little to weaken the popular prejudice against "greedy munitions interests."
</p><p>The hearings overlapped the 73rd and <a href="/wiki/Seventy-fourth_United_States_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventy-fourth United States Congress">74th</a> Congresses. They only came to an end after Chairman Nye provoked the Democratic caucus into cutting off funding. Nye, in the last hearing the Committee held in early 1936, attacked former Democratic President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, suggesting that Wilson had withheld essential information from Congress as it considered a <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_war_by_the_United_States" title="Declaration of war by the United States">declaration of war</a>. Democratic leaders, including <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senate_Committee_on_Appropriations" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Senate Committee on Appropriations">Appropriations Committee</a> Chairman <a href="/wiki/Carter_Glass" title="Carter Glass">Carter Glass</a> of <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, unleashed a furious response against Nye for "dirtdaubing the <a href="/wiki/Sepulcher" class="mw-redirect" title="Sepulcher">sepulcher</a> of Woodrow Wilson." Standing before cheering colleagues in a packed Senate chamber, Glass slammed his fist onto his desk in protest until blood dripped from his knuckles, effectively prompting the Democratic caucus to withhold all funding for further hearings.
</p><p>Although the "Nye Committee" failed to achieve its goal of nationalizing the arms industry, it inspired three congressional neutrality acts in the mid-1930s that signaled profound American opposition to overseas involvement.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_summary">Party summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Party summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For details, see <i><a href="#Changes_in_membership">Changes in membership</a></i>, below.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senate">Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There were 48 states with two Senators per state, this gave the Senate 96 seats. Membership changed with four deaths, one resignation, and two appointees who were replaced by electees.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><small><b>Section contents:</b> <a href="#Senate_2">Senate</a>: <a href="#Majority_(Democratic)_leadership">Majority (D)</a>, <a href="#Minority_(Republican)_leadership">Minority (R)</a> • <a href="#House_of_Representatives_2">House</a>: <a href="#Majority_(Democratic)_leadership_2">Majority (D)</a>,  <a href="#Minority_(Republican)_leadership_2">Minority (R)</a></small>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senate_2">Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="President of the United States Senate">President</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Nance_Garner" title="John Nance Garner">John Nance Garner</a> (D)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/President_pro_tempore_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="President pro tempore of the United States Senate">President pro tempore</a>: <a href="/wiki/Key_Pittman" title="Key Pittman">Key Pittman</a> (D)</li></ul>
<h4><span id="Majority_.28Democratic.29_leadership"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Majority_(Democratic)_leadership">Majority (Democratic) leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Majority (Democratic) leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Majority_Leader_of_the_United_States_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Leader of the United States Senate">Majority Leader</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Democratic_Conference_Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate Democratic Conference Chairman">Democratic Conference Chairman</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_T._Robinson" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph T. Robinson">Joseph T. Robinson</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Majority_Whip_of_the_United_States_Senate" class="mw-redirect" title="Majority Whip of the United States Senate">Assistant Majority Leader (Majority Whip)</a>: <a href="/wiki/J._Hamilton_Lewis" title="J. Hamilton Lewis">J. Hamilton Lewis</a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Members">Members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senate_3">Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=73rd_United_States_Congress&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Senators are popularly elected statewide every two years, with one-third beginning new six-year terms with each Congress. Preceding the names in the list below are <a href="/wiki/Classes_of_United_States_Senators" class="mw-redirect" title="Classes of United States Senators">Senate class numbers</a>, which indicate the cycle of their election, In this Congress, Class 1 meant their term ended with this Congress, requiring reelection in 1934; Class 2 meant their term began in the last Congress, requiring reelection in 1936; and Class 3 meant their term began in this Congress, requiring reelection in 1938.
</p>
